433 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


State v. Richardson


No. 49A02-0807-CR-583


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


906 N.E.2d 263; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 891



May 22, 2009, Decided 
May 22, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Richardson, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 3065 (Ind. Ct. App., July 27, 2009)
Transfer granted by, Vacated by State v. Richardson, 919 N.E.2d 554, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1351 (Ind., 2009)
Superseded by, Remanded by State v. Richardson, 927 N.E.2d 379, 2010 Ind. LEXIS 380 (Ind., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable William Young, Judge. Cause No. 49G20-0707-FA-135662.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendant with Class A felony dealing in cocaine, Class C felony possession of cocaine, Class C felony possession of cocaine and a firearm, Class A misdemeanor resisting law enforcement, and Class A misdemeanor battery on a law enforcement officer. The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) granted defendant's motion to suppress and denied the State's motion to correct error. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the trial court erred in granting defendant's motion to suppress, because it claimed that defendant was properly searched incident to his lawful arrest for carrying a handgun and resisting law enforcement. As admitted by defendant, he was initially stopped for a seatbelt violation. The issue before the appellate court was whether the officer permissibly questioned defendant about an unusual bulge in his pants pocket. The appellate court concluded that such a question was not unreasonable. While defendant admitted it was a handgun and produced a permit, the officer had probable cause to arrest defendant after checking with headquarters three separate times and being told that defendant had a felony record, which would invalidate the permit. While the information the officer received was, in fact, incorrect, that did not justify exclusion of the evidence obtained during a search incident to defendant's arrest. Without any indication that the police were reckless in maintaining their records or knowingly made false entries in order to justify false arrests, the appellate court concluded that exclusion was not justified.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

434 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Whatley v. State


No. 49A02-0809-CR-808


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


906 N.E.2d 259; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 837



May 21, 2009, Decided 
May 21, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer granted by, Vacated by Whatley v. State, 919 N.E.2d 549, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1069 (Ind., 2009)
Superseded by Whatley v. State, 2010 Ind. LEXIS 382 (Ind., June 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Michael Jensen, Magistrate. Cause No. 49G20-0803-FD-064067.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of possession of cocaine in a quantity in excess of three grams within one thousand feet of a "youth program center" as a Class A felony under Ind. Code § 35-48-4-6(b)(3)(B)(iv). On appeal, he claimed that the criminal statute and Ind. Code § 35-41-1-29, the statute defining a "youth program center," were unconstitutionally vague as applied to him.


OVERVIEW: In terms of felony enhancement for violation of a "drug free zone," the provision including a "youth program center" as such a zone, had not yet been the subject of an Indiana decision. The cases from other jurisdictions were not uniform in their analysis or in their results. Nevertheless, the court found persuasive guidance from several representative cases which held that the principal character and use of a structure was not changed by some ancillary or accessory use. The court held that the community church was and remained a church and was not converted into a youth program center by reason of its faith-based activities for young people. Bi-weekly Girl Scout troop meetings and mentoring of children by adult members of the congregation were accessory or incidental to the existence and identity as a church. The church was not a youth program center. It remained a church notwithstanding the incidental activities not solely religious in nature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause to the trial court for entry of a conviction as a Class C felony and to sentence defendant accordingly.

 

435 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Salter v. State


No. 49A02-0808-CR-672


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


906 N.E.2d 212; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 831



May 20, 2009, Decided 
May 20, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT, CRIMINAL DIVISION, ROOM 1. The Honorable Tanya Walton Pratt, Judge. Cause No. 49G01-0611-FC-212949.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court, Criminal Division Room 1 (Indiana), entered final judgments of conviction after it found defendant guilty of 40 counts of child exploitation, Class C felonies, Ind. Code § 35-42-4-4, and five counts of dissemination of matter harmful to minors, Class D felonies, Ind. Code § 35-49-3-3. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Indiana police received information from Delaware authorities that defendant, an Indiana resident, was communicating in a sexual way with a girl in Delaware who was 16-years-old but under the age of 18. Police executed a search warrant at his house. Based on what police discovered, he was charged with child exploitation regarding digital images of the naked girl, child exploitation for digital images of other naked children, and dissemination of matter harmful to minors based on digital images of his genitals he sent to the girl. The trial court found him guilty after a bench trial on 40 counts of child exploitation and five counts of dissemination. The appellate court found that the child exploitation convictions could not stand because Ind. Code § 35-42-4-4 was concerned with creation of the material, and not the downloading of images from a computer and saving copies of them to a CD. It also found that the dissemination convictions had to be reversed because he did not have fair notice that pictures of his genitals were patently offensive to prevailing standards in the adult community as a whole, especially since minors in Indiana could consent to sex at the age of 16.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgments of conviction.

 

436 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Jacobsville Developers East, LLC v. Warrick County


No. 87A01-0809-CV-420


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


905 N.E.2d 1034; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 813



May 5, 2009, Decided 
May 5, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Jacobsville Developers East LLC v. Warrick County, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1059 (Ind., Aug. 19, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE WARRICK SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Robert R. Aylsworth, Judge. Cause No. 87D02-0712-PL-501.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner appealed a judgment by the Warrick Superior Court (Indiana) that dismissed its complaint for inverse condemnation against defendants, county board and area planning commission (APC), for lack of subject matter jurisdiction; the owner claimed that it was not required to seek and complete the certiorari review process before initiating its inverse condemnation action.


OVERVIEW: The owner's first application for approval of a two-lot subdivision was denied by the APC because it failed to comply with an ordinance's dedication provision. The owner sought certiorari relief. However, it dismissed the action and filed a second application with the APC for approval of a plat that included a dedicated right-of-way in lieu of a setback. Thereafter, the owner sued the county and the APC alleging that the ordinance's dedication requirement constituted a taking without just compensation. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the owner was seeking to avoid the dedication provision, not to be compensated for an actual taking. Therefore, certiorari review would not have been futile. The certiorari court could have taken evidence to supplement the record submitted and entered a declaratory judgment affirming, reversing, or modifying the APC's decision. Therefore, by failing to fully pursue the judicial review remedy available in the certiorari court, the owner failed to exhaust its available administrative remedies. As a result, the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction to hear the owner's substantive exaction claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

437 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Alvies v. State


No. 49A02-0808-CR-710


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


905 N.E.2d 57; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 742



April 30, 2009, Decided 
April 30, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. CRIMINAL DIVISION, ROOM 2. The Honorable Robert R. Altice, Jr., Judge. Cause No. 49G02-0702-MR-31203.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of murder, a felony, under Ind. Code § 35-42-1-1; rape, as a Class A felony, under Ind. Code § 35-42-4-1; criminal confinement, as a Class B felony, under Ind. Code § 35-42-3-3; burglary, as a Class B felony, under Ind. Code § 35-43-2-1; and auto theft, as a Class D felony, under Ind. Code § 35-43-4-2.5. The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) sentenced him to a total executed sentence of 110 years. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support his convictions for murder and auto theft, and the finding that he was armed with a deadly weapon when he committed rape and criminal confinement. The appellate court found the evidence sufficient. As for the murder, defendant admitted he had something to do with it and the victim's murder was found to be a probable and natural consequence of defendant's action in taking the victim to the woods and raping her. The fact that defendant was in the car when it left the murder scene supported a finding that he was at least an accomplice to auto theft. The determination that defendant was armed with a deadly weapon was supported by evidence that the gun was pulled when the victim opened the door and the fact that she was killed with a gun at the end of the ordeal, allowing the jury to concluded that defendant and the others had at least constructive possession of the gun at all times. While use of defendant's juvenile adjudications as an aggravator was error, his sentence was upheld based on the fact that the offenses occurred while defendant was on suspended committed and based on the nature and circumstances of the crimes.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

438 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Founds. of East Chi., Inc. v. City of E. Chicago


No. 49A02-0711-CV-987


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


905 N.E.2d 30; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 736



April 28, 2009, Decided 
April 28, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer granted by, Vacated by Founds. of E. Chi. v. Ag & E. Chi., 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1047 (Ind., Aug. 21, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable S. K. Reid, Judge. Cause No. 49D13-070.5-PL-19348.
City of E. Chicago v. E. Chi. Second Century, Inc., 878 N.E.2d 358, 2007 Ind. App. LEXIS 2934 (Ind. Ct. App., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant not-for-profit entities sued appellee city, challenging the constitutionality of Ind. Code § 4-33-6-7. Appellee State eventually intervened to defend the statute's validity. Following a bench trial, the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) issued an order entering final judgment in favor of the city, denying the not-for-profit entities' requested relief of a permanent injunction. The non-for-profit entities appealed.


OVERVIEW: Initially, the city and entered into letter agreements with a gaming license applicant which provided that the applicant would pay a percentage of its gross receipts to the not-for-profit entities as local economic development funds. Later, after an amendment to § 4-33-6-7, and pursuant to the authority granted by that section, the city passed an ordinance to redirect to itself all of the money that the casino had been paying to the not-for-profit entities and its predecessors. The not-for-profit entities argued that § 4-33-6-7 was invalid. However, the appellate court held that, even if that were true, the not-for-profit entities were not entitled to the requested relief. The city always retained the authority to change the recipient of the licensee's local economic development funds and to modify the arrangement encapsulated in the letter agreements, regardless of the constitutionality of § 4-33-6-7. In other words, the licensee was obligated to support the community at a certain level, but it was left to the city to determine the identity of the payees, and the city had the authority to pass a new ordinance changing the identity of the payees at any time.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


 

439 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Donaldson v. State


No. 71A03-0811-CR-564


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


904 N.E.2d 294; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 673



April 13, 2009, Decided 
April 13, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE ST. JOSEPH SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Jerome Frese, Judge. Cause No. 71D03-0803-FD-230.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The St. Joseph Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of violating Ind. Code § 9-30-10-16, operating a motor vehicle while privileges were suspended. On appeal, he claimed that § 9-30-10-16(b)'s rebuttable presumption that defendant knew his license was suspended unconstitutionally removed the burden of proof from the State. He also challenged the sufficiency of the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Although defendant waived his constitutional challenge, the court considered it and found that Ind. Code § 9-30-10-16(b) had to be read to declare that, upon proof of one fact, service of a suspension by first class mail at a defendant's last known address, defendant's knowledge of the suspension was presumed or inferred, but this presumption was rebuttable. There was a rational connection between service to a defendant and his knowledge. There was no abuse of discretion in admitting defendant's incomplete driving record into evidence. Defendant's offer of his Notice of Reinstatement did not rebut the presumption of knowledge. Sufficient evidence supported the conviction. The State proved that the Bureau of Motor Vehicles (BMV) sent a notice of suspension for being a habitual traffic violator to defendant's address last shown in the BMV records. Defendant tried to rebut the presumption of knowledge with evidence that other notices from the BMV may have caused him to be confused; but, that evidence did not rebut the presumption because defendant presented no evidence that he had attempted to contact the BMV to determine the status of his driving privileges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction.

 

440 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Lacy v. State


No. 31A04-0810-CR-571


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


903 N.E.2d 486; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 526



March 24, 2009, Decided 
March 24, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Lacy v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 606 (Ind., May 21, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE HARRISON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Curtis Eskew, Special Judge. Cause No. 31D01-0409-CM-762.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Harrison Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of possession of a knife with an automatic opening blade as a class B misdemeanor under Ind. Code § 35-47-5-2. She appealed, arguing that the statute violated her right to bear arms under Ind. Const. art. I, § 32.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutional on its face. The court stated that a court should focus on the actual operation of the statute at issue and refrain from speculating upon hypothetical applications; thus, it would focus on whether the statute's application in defendant's case was unconstitutional. It stated that Ind. Code § 35-47-5-2 was rationally calculated to advance the public good and thus was a valid exercise of the State's police power. Furthermore, switchblades, the type of knife that defendant possessed, were not typically possessed by law-abiding citizens for self-defense purposes. Moreover, § 35-47-5-2 was limited because it did not prohibit the possession of all knives, but only knives that opened automatically or could be propelled by hand pressure applied to a button, device containing gas, spring, or other device in the handle of the knife. Based upon these conclusions, § 35-47-5-2 did not place a material burden upon the core value of defendant's right to defend herself. Thus, § 35-47-5-2 was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

441 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Timberlake, Inc. v. O'Brien


No. 46A03-0803-CV-134


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


902 N.E.2d 843; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 517



March 13, 2009, Decided 
March 13, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE LAPORTE CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Thomas Alevizos, Judge. Cause No. 46C01-0406-PL-191.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The LaPorte Circuit Court (Indiana) denied appellant land owner's motion for summary judgment to quiet title to a railroad-right of-way and for trespass. The owner claimed error in finding that a railroad conveyed a right-of-way easement rather than a fee interest in the property. Appellee grantee under a quitclaim deed from the railroad cross-appealed the finding that he held an easement rather than a fee interest in the property.


OVERVIEW: The railroad had filed its intent of abandonment with the Interstate Commerce Commission (ICC). Three 19th century handwritten deeds to the railroad contained, along with the term "a strip of land for a right of way," language indicating the purpose for which the land was to be used. The court interpreted the deeds as conveying a right of way that was an easement, not a fee. Although the use of the temporal descriptor "forever" favored construction of the deed as conveying a fee simple absolute to the railroad, such language was merely a factor in determining whether the parties intended to grant a fee or an easement. Absent some record of the value of the land in 1881, the consideration paid was not conclusive as to the parties' intent. The ICC had not issued a certificate authorizing abandonment, and the railroad did not remove the tracks until July of 1991; thus, at the time the railroad quitclaimed the property to the grantee, the railroad had not yet statutorily abandoned the property and could convey its interest in the property to the grantee. As such, the trial court properly determined that the grantee held an easement for a railroad right-of-way over the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


 

442 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Bledsoe's, Inc. v. Steuben Lakes Reg'l Waste Dist.


No. 76A03-0806-CV-272


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 364



March 6, 2009, Decided 
March 6, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
APPEAL FROM THE STEUBEN CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Allen N. Wheat, Judge. Cause No. 76C01-0603-CC-101.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Steuben Circuit Court (Indiana) granted summary judgment to appellee regional waste district in the district's complaint to enforce a sewer rate increase ordinance against appellants, the owner of a mobile home park and the owner of a campground. The circuit court also denied the campground owner's counterclaim.


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that Steuben Lakes Regional Waste District, Ind., Ordinance No. 2002-03, 2003-06, and 2005-06 were unconstitutionally vague for not defining "mobile home park" or "trailer park." The court found that: 1) the users of the system were split into classes based on location of the properties; 2) each class was broken into five categories-- residential, commercial, governmental, institutional, and industrial; 3) the terms "mobile home park" and "trailer park" were used interchangeably--the terms were always found in the residential category of each class, but if one of the two terms existed, the other did not; 4) the ordinance's goals and policies were to recoup the costs of installing the new system while charging users for the waste they generated. The court found that the ordinances were sufficiently specific to provide fair warning to users. However, the District'cs claim that the campground owner was properly billed for debt services remained at issue. That owner discharged waste into the sewers although not connected; therefore, the liens levied for unpaid sewer bills were at least bloated, and it was error to grant summary judgment to the District on that claim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as to constitutionality of the ordinance, reversed the judgment as to the billing status of the campground owner and remanded the cause to the trial court to determine what portion of the monthly bills that were not paid were for debt services, and therefore properly billed to him.


 

443 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Byers v. Quillen


No. 29A05-0805-CV-307


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 184



January 30, 2009, Decided 
January 30, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Byers v. Quillen, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1282 (Ind., July 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
APPEAL FROM THE HAMILTON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Daniel J. Pfleging, Judge. Cause No. 29D02-0710-PL-1233.
Byers v. Quillen, 2009 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 180 (Ind. Ct. App., Jan. 30, 2009)
Byers v. Quillen, 901 N.E.2d 82, 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 142 (Ind. Ct. App., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient filed with the Indiana Department of Insurance a medical malpractice complaint against defendant doctor. The doctor filed a motion for a preliminary determination of law on the question of the timeliness of the complaint. The Hamilton Superior Court (Indiana) denied the motion. The doctor filed an interlocutory appeal.


OVERVIEW: The patient complained to the doctor, an internal medicine speciality, about bubbles or crackling in her ears, dizziness, and/or a lack of coordination. The doctor attributed the symptoms to sinusitis or stress. The patient informed her psychiatrist of the assessments made by the doctor and the psychiatrist ordered an MRI, which revealed a tumor growing out of the balance and hearing nerves. The patient underwent surgery and ultimately could not close her left eye, had left facial nerve palsy, and permanently lost hearing in her left ear. The patient filed her complaint exactly two years after her surgery. The issue on appeal was whether the patient filed her complaint within the two-year statute of limitations for medical malpractice actions in Ind. Code § 34-18-7-1(b) (2004). The appellate court concluded that the trial court erred when it denied the doctor's request for summary judgment based upon the statute of limitations. The last possible date on which the doctor could have rendered a diagnosis supporting the patient's claim was the last time the patient complained to the doctor, a date that was more than two years before the patient filed her claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

444 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Wood v. Walden


No. 49A02-0806-CV-556


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


899 N.E.2d 728; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 22



January 20, 2009, Decided 
January 20, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Robyn L. Moberly, Judge. Cause No. 49D12-0712-CT-051253.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, individually and as guardian of his daughter, who was injured in a car accident, challenged a decision of the Marion County Superior Court (Indiana) that denied his motion for partial summary judgment and granted summary judgment and final judgment in favor of appellee-intervenor State on the guardian's action to declare the punitive damages statute, Ind. Code § 34-51-3-6, unconstitutional. The State cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The complaint sought treble damages, attorney's fees and costs under Ind. Code § 35-24-3 et seq. The State argued that the trial court should not have ruled on the merits of the guardian's claim because punitive damages had not been awarded, and the guardian lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute. The court agreed. The guardian suffered no injury as to the allocation of a punitive damage award because he had not obtained a determination on liability, let alone a determination that he was entitled to punitive damages. The existence of such an award was purely speculative at this point in the proceedings. Until a jury announced a punitive damages award, neither party had a right to any portion of such an award. A record containing the facts essential to establish actual harm was ascertainable only if a trial was held, instructions were given, a jury found for the guardian and awarded punitive damages, the punitive damages were paid to the Court Clerk, and 75% of the award was then paid into the State's Violent Crimes Victims Compensation Fund. Because these events had not occurred, there was no justiciable controversy. Thus, summary judgment was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


 

445 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Lindsey v. DeGroot


No. 35A02-0805-CV-470


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


898 N.E.2d 1251; 2009 Ind. App. LEXIS 6



January 12, 2009, Decided 
January 12, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE HUNTINGTON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Frederick A. Schurger, Special Judge. Cause No. 35D01-0312-PL-262.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant neighbors challenged an order of the Huntington Superior Court (Indiana) that granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, a farming operation and its owner, claiming error in interpreting and applying the Indiana Right to Farm Act and claiming that issues of material fact remained as to the neighbors' nuisance, criminal trespass, criminal mischief, and intentional infliction of emotional distress claims against appellees.


OVERVIEW: The farm was an agricultural operation for the purposes of the Indiana Right to Farm Act, Ind. Code § 32-30-6-1 (2003). The court rejected the claim that the Act was unconstitutional because it granted an easement to the farm over the neighbors' land. The land at issue was owned by the farm, and the neighbors did not designate any evidence to rebut the surveyor's affidavit. The claim that the farm's operation was a nuisance that interfered with the neighbors' use and enjoyment of their land was rejected because the Act barred the suit. There was no allegation that the alleged nuisance resulted from negligent operation. Assuming that the farm committed statutory violations alleged in a preliminary injunction and that the farm owed a statutory duty to the neighbors, the statutory violations could have been considered evidence of negligence, but the neighbors did not show that the violations proximately caused their injuries or that injuries were foreseeable. The neighbors designated no evidence establishing reckless, knowing, or intentional behavior by the farm, and the farm's actions were not "outrageous" behavior.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment.

 

446 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Gibson v. Ind. Dep't of Corr.


No. 49A04-0803-CV-165


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


899 N.E.2d 40; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2613



December 29, 2008, Decided 
December 29, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Gibson v. Ind. Dep't. of Corr., 915 N.E.2d 987, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 452 (Ind., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Robyn Moberly, Judge. Cause No. 49D12-0708-PL-34622.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a certified class of violent offenders, sued appellees, a certified class of state agencies, claiming that inclusion on the Ind. Code § 36-2-13-5.5 registry violated Ind. Const. art. 1, §§ 12, 23. The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) entered a preliminary injunction as to the enforcement of lifetime registration. The offenders challenged a 10-year registration requirement. The agencies challenged the bar against lifetime registration.


OVERVIEW: The court found that those who committed lesser crimes that resulted in death did not demonstrate intentional violent deadly behavior toward another person that required compliance with the Registry. There were sufficient inherent differences between murder, voluntary manslaughter, and attempts to commit those crimes, as compared with the other listed offenses resulting in death, to permit the General Assembly to specify different treatment. As such, § 36-2-13-5.5 was constitutional and did not violate § 23. Therefore, the offenders' preliminary injunction argument failed on the first prong, likelihood of success on the merits. Because there was some recidivism among violent offenders after release and community notification about violent offenders was an opportunity for enhancing public safety, the registration requirement for violent offenders met the low threshold of rational relation and did not violate § 12. Ind. Code § 11-8-8-19(d) applied only to sex offenders; accordingly, a violent offender had to comply with the Registry for ten years, unless the offender also fell within Ind. Code § 11-8-8-19(b), (c), or (e), in which case lifetime registration was warranted.


OUTCOME: To the extent that the trial court's order granted a preliminary injunction against lifetime registration for all violent offenders, the court reversed and remanded the case with instructions to clarify the preliminary injunction consistent with this opinion. In all other respects, the court affirmed the trial court.


 

447 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Houston v. State


No. 73A01-0805-CR-223


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


898 N.E.2d 358; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2575



December 16, 2008, Decided 
December 16, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Houston v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 147 (Ind., Feb. 26, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE SHELBY SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Jack H. Tandy, Judge. Cause No. 73D01-0602-FC-0006.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Shelby Superior Court (Indiana) that convicted him of operating a motor vehicle while privileges were forfeited for life, a Class C felony, Ind. Code § 9-30-10-17. He argued that evidence obtained as a result of the traffic stop should not have been admitted and that § 9-30-10-17 was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the challenged evidence was the fruit of an illegal search and seizure because the police improperly stopped his car for a dangling license plate, a violation of Ind. Code § 9-18-2-26. He claimed that his license plate was secured by two bolts and was not "swinging," as contemplated by § 9-18-2-26(b)(1). The court found that the movement of the plate, as described by the deputy, fell within the common definition of "swinging." Even though the deputy discerned the plate information and called it in to dispatch, a safety issue was raised by a non-secure license plate. The deputy was concerned about the plate becoming completely detached and injuring a person or damaging property. Protecting citizens from possible injury was a legitimate public policy objective. The court held that § 9-18-2-26 was not unconstitutionally vague because the ordinary meaning of the word "swinging" was understood by ordinary people and the term did not encourage arbitrary enforcement. The court concluded that the traffic stop was proper and that there was no error in admitting the evidence obtained as a result of the stop.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction.

 

448 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Studler v. Ind. BMV


No. 49A02-0804-CV-385


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


896 N.E.2d 1156; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2634



November 17, 2008, Decided 
November 17, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Ordered published by Studler v. Ind. BMV, 896 N.E.2d 1156, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2625 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Gary L. Miller, Judge. Cause Nos. 49D05-0704-PL-16603.
Studler v. Ind. BMV, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 3288 (Ind. Ct. App., Nov. 17, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged an order of the Marion Superior Court, which denied his motion for summary judgment and granted appellee Indiana Bureau of Motor Vehicles (BMV) summary judgment in his action seeking to a declaration that Ind. Code §§ 9-18-24.5-4 and 9-29-5-34.5 was unconstitutional as violating Ind. Const. art. I, § 23.


OVERVIEW: The driver purchased an "Environment" license plate. He paid an administrative fee and a group fee in addition to regular registration fees. In order to continue displaying the license plate on his vehicle, the driver had to continue to pay his regular registration fees plus the additional fees when he renewed his vehicle registration. The court of appeals held that §§ 9-18-24.5-4 and 9-29-5-34.5, which offered the "In God We Trust" license plate without the requirement of paying the administrative fee, were constitutional. The General Assembly's classification system was not arbitrary but was reasonably related to the inherent characteristics of the license plates. Ind. Code chapter 25 license plates imposed additional burdens on the BMV justifying payment of the administrative fee. Based on those additional ministerial tasks, the legislative classification of license plates requiring payment of the administrative fee was reasonably related to the inherent characteristics of the license plates in the class. The requirement of paying the administrative fee was equally applicable to all chapter 25 license plates and did not apply to any non-chapter 25 license plates.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment.

Loveless v. State


No. 39A01-0802-PC-78


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


896 N.E.2d 918; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2534



November 14, 2008, Decided 
November 14, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Loveless v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 113 (Ind., Jan. 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Ted R. Todd, Judge. Cause No. 39C01-9201-CF-4.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Jefferson Circuit Court (Indiana) that denied her postconviction petition; defendant claimed that her counsel was ineffective for pressuring her to sign the plea agreement, that she was a minor who could not enter into a plea agreement, and that the Indiana Penal Code was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was 16 when she committed her crimes and when she agreed to plead guilty to them. She was represented by counsel, but no guardian ad litem was appointed for her. Her mother was apparently present when defendant agreed to plead guilty, but she did not sign the agreement for defendant. The appellate court found that defendant did not direct it to evidence in the record that she was a protected person. Accordingly, she could not void her plea agreement on the ground she was a minor. Defendant's counsel were not ineffective for failing to anticipate a change in the law. Finally, defendant waived her challenge to the constitutionality of the Penal Code because she did not raise it during her trial or on direct appeal. Therefore, her postconviction petition was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

LHT Capital, LLC. v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm'n


No. 49A02-0712-CV-1149


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


895 N.E.2d 124; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2387



October 23, 2008, Decided 
October 23, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by LHT Capital v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm., 2009 Ind. LEXIS 171 (Ind., Feb. 26, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Gerald S. Zore, Judge. Cause No. 49D07-0709-PL-39946.
LHT Capital, LLC v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm'n, 891 N.E.2d 646, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1680 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought rehearing of a published opinion in which the court affirmed a decision of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) that granted appellee racing commission's motion to dismiss for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. The court had held that appellant did not exhaust administrative remedies by raising the constitutionality and legality of 71 Ind. Admin. Code § 11-1-13(d) (Emergency Rule) before the commission.


OVERVIEW: The parties had entered into a settlement resolving an initial dispute without confronting the broader legal issues. Having accepted the benefits of the arrangement, appellant could not argue on appeal that the Emergency Rule was invalid and unconstitutional and that appellant was entitled to a refund of a $ 9 million transfer fee. Appellant could have notified the commission of its reservation of rights to challenge the Rule, filed a declaratory judgment suit and sought expedited handling (due to an impending deadline for a $ 250 million license fee), or sought emergency injunctive relief. Appellant did none of those, instead electing to mislead the commission about its agreement with the transfer fee so appellant could benefit from the commission's favorable decision by reaping a $ 40 million profit. Even if appellant had prevailed, remand would have been necessary because the settlement included specific provisions purporting to prevent appellant from bringing this action and purporting to release claims against the commission and release claims for a refund from the commission. The trial court would have had to consider the settlement's language before ordering a refund money.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for rehearing.


 

451 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Bailey v. Ind. Newspapers, Inc. (In re T.B.)


No. 49A02-0712-JV-1007


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


895 N.E.2d 321; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2527



October 21, 2008, Decided 
October 21, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Marilyn Moores, Judge. Cause Nos. 49D09-0605-JC-21830 and 49D09-0705-JC-19282.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee newspaper sought access to records in child-in-need-of-services (CHINS) and juvenile delinquency proceedings involving appellant mother, who was charged with the murder of her child. The Marion Superior Court, Juvenile Division (Indiana) granted the newspaper access to redacted copies of records. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court first held that Ind. Code § 31-39-2-10 applied to both closed and pending cases and to both CHINS and juvenile delinquency cases. It held that the trial court properly granted access to court records in both a closed CHINS case and one that was pending at the time of the child's death. Furthermore, the death of a child was a matter of public interest. The trial court erred in releasing a transcript of a review hearing in the pending CHINS case. A "proceeding" in Ind. Code § 31-32-6-2 was an actual hearing or trial. Moreover, the statute did not say that a trial court could release a transcript of a proceeding from which the public was excluded. The trial court erred under Ind. Code § 31-39-2-8 in granting access to records of the mother's juvenile delinquency proceedings. Her alleged neglect and murder of the child were not the bases for the delinquency allegations. Finally, under Ind. Code § 31-33-18-1.5 the trial court properly granted the newspaper access to Department of Child Services records involving the child. The mother did not argue that the child's death was not the result of abuse, abandonment, or neglect or that the records were otherwise confidential.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders releasing a transcript of a review hearing in the pending CHINS case and allowing the newspaper access to the mother's juvenile delinquency records. It affirmed the trial court's other orders.

Silverman v. Villegas

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Miller v. Villegas, 130 S. Ct. 400, 175 L. Ed. 2d 270, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7353 (U.S., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Patrick McCarty, Judge. Cause No. 49D03-0208-PL-1448.
Villegas v. Silverman, 832 N.E.2d 598, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 1451 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court (Indiana), on remand, entered summary judgment for plaintiff illegal aliens. The illegal aliens' had filed a class action against defendant motor vehicle bureau commissioner to challenge recently implemented identification requirements needed to obtain other identification documents. It court then awarded attorney fees and costs to them as "prevailing parties" under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988. The commissioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The illegal aliens sued the commissioner claiming they were unable to obtain driver's licenses and/or identification cards because of identification requirements implemented by the bureau. In a prior appeal, the appellate court held that the identification requirements were an administrative rule not promulgated pursuant to required state statutory law regarding administrative rules. It so concluded because it found that (1) they succeeded on their claim pursuant to the Indiana Administrative Rules and Procedures Act, Ind. OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


 

454 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Pavese v. Cleaning Solutions


No. 93A02-0803-EX-284


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


894 N.E.2d 570; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2120



September 30, 2008, Decided 
September 30, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE INDIANA WORKER'S COMPENSATION BOARD. Cause No. C-18135.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant alleged she sustained an injury while working for appellee employer. The Indiana Worker's Compensation Board denied her request for benefits, finding she failed to prove her injury arose out of her employment. The claimant appealed, contending that Ind. Code § 22-3-2-2(a), which placed the burden of proof on employees, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The claimant was found unconscious on the floor. She did not remember the events leading up to her fall, but testified the floor was sometimes slick due to oil and water. Her treating physician opined that she fell backwards, causing a concussion with retrograde amnesia. He diagnosed a syncopal episode, but could not rule out the claimant's slipping on the floor. A single hearing member found she experienced a personal event on the job which, while unexplained, was not covered by the Indiana Worker's Compensation Act. The Board affirmed. The claimant argued that § 22-3-2-2(a) was unconstitutional as applied to her because gave her the burden of proving a negative, i.e., that her injury was not the result of a personal health condition. The appellate court held that as the positional risk doctrine announced in Milledge v. The Oaks was not constitutionally mandated, § 22-3-2-2(a), which abrogated that doctrine, was constitutional. As the claimant presented two possibilities for her fall--a syncopal episode (a personal risk not covered by worker's compensation) and a slippery floor (a neutral risk which was covered)--and she bore the burden of proof, her claim was properly denied.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Micheau v. State


No. 38A05-0710-CR-578


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


893 N.E.2d 1053; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2029



September 16, 2008, Decided 
September 16, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Micheau v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 1639 (Ind., Jan. 8, 2009)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by, Decision reached on appeal by Micheau v. State, 2017 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 442 (Ind. Ct. App., Apr. 10, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE JAY CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Brian D. Hutchinson, Judge. Cause No. 38C01-0706-FB-4.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Jay Circuit Court (Indiana) that convicted him of drug charges, violations of Ind. Code §§ 35-48-4-1.1; 35-48-4-6.1; 35-48-4-14.5 (2006) and Ind. Code §§ 35-48-4-11; 35-47-5-4.1; 35-41-5-1 (2004). Defendant challenged the admission of evidence obtained in a search, the constitutionality of § 35-48-4-1.1(a)(2) and violations of double jeopardy and Ind. Code § 35-38-1-6; and Ind. Const. art. I, § 16.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was on parole in Indiana for a Texas marijuana conviction. He claimed that the search was an investigatory search that was under the guise of a probationary search. The court disagreed, finding that the initial search of the residence was a true parole search and that the search was reasonable. The court found that Ind. Code § 35-48-4-1.1(a)(2) was not unconstitutionally vague because it adequately advised the public of the proscribed conduct, namely possessing methamphetamine with the intent to manufacture methamphetamine. Convictions for § 35-48-4-1.1, § 35-48-4-14.5 and § 35-41-5-1 did not violate the prohibition against double jeopardy and Ind. Code § 35-38-1-6 because the evidence permitted a reasonable conclusion that two independent offenses were committed for which defendant could be separately punished; the attempt charge, was not a lesser included offense of dealing in methamphetamine. Convictions for § 35-48-4-1.1; § 35-48-4-14.5 and § 35-41-5-1 did not violate the proportionality clause because they were not the same crime; therefore different penalties were appropriate. Section 35-48-4-6.1 was a lesser included offense of § 35-48-4-1.1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for dealing in methamphetamine; dealing in a sawed off shotgun; possession of marijuana; and attempted dealing in methamphetamine. The court vacated the convictions and sentences entered for possession of methamphetamine and possession of chemical reagents or precursors with intent to manufacture a controlled substance.

A pat-down search incident to arrest was proper because there was probable cause for the arrest.

Baumgartner v. State


No. 53A05-0708-CR-463


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


891 N.E.2d 1131; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1788



August 15, 2008, Decided 
August 15, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MONROE CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Kenneth G. Todd, Judge. Cause No. 53C03-0601-FA-35.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Monroe Circuit Court of Class D felony performing sexual conduct in the presence of a minor under Ind. Code § 35-42-4-5(c) (2004) and his sentence, following the denial of his motion for dismissal pursuant to Ind. R. Crim. P. 4(C).


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that defendant was not without fault in his counsel's withdrawal; that delay was charged to defendant. Defendant did not object to the State's request for a continuance, even though the trial date was then beyond the Rule 4(C) limit. Defendant waived his right to a speedy trial. Section 35-42-4-5(c) was not void for vagueness. Defendant's hypothetical claims could not demonstrate vagueness. A person of ordinary intelligence would not doubt that the conduct of defendant, who while standing in a doorway masturbated while looking at the children in a room across the hall, was prohibited. Defendant's claim that he did not touch or fondle himself "in the presence" of a child because there was no evidence that the children were aware of defendant's conduct had been rejected. Finally, the evidence supported the conviction. A witness's testimony was not inherently contradictory or coerced, and the "incredible dubiosity" rule did not apply. The witness testified that she saw defendant standing in a doorway across the hall from the bedroom where her two children were in bed, and that defendant was masturbating while looking at the children.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

LHT Capital, LLC v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm'n


No. 49A02-0712-CV-1149


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


891 N.E.2d 646; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1680



August 7, 2008, Decided 
August 7, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by LHT Capital, LLC. v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm'n, 895 N.E.2d 124, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2387 (Ind. Ct. App., 2008)
Transfer denied by LHT Capital v. Ind. Horse Racing Comm., 2009 Ind. LEXIS 171 (Ind., Feb. 26, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Gerald S. Zore, Judge. Cause No. 49D07-0709-PL-39946.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an interest holder in a race track, challenged a decision of the Marion Superior Court (Indiana) that granted the appellees', the state horse racing commission, the race track, and other interest holders, motion to dismiss appellant's complaint regarding a transfer fee assessed in connection with an agreement to redeem appellant's interest in the track pursuant to 71 Ind. Admin. Code § 11-1-13.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimed error in finding that it had not exhausted administrative remedies. The court disagreed, finding that the timely filing of a petition for judicial review under Ind. Code § 4-21.5-5-2(a) was only one of the requirements for seeking judicial review. Exhaustion was required under Ind. Code § 4-21.5-5-4(a). To the extent that appellant raised the constitutionality of the Emergency Rule (71 Ind. Admin. Code § 11-1-13(d)) in a letter to the commission's counsel, that action was insufficient to exhaust administrative remedies; the issue was not raised before the commission at a hearing. Appellant also failed to show that presentation of the issue to the commission was futile. Appellant filed its petition and negotiated an agreement allowing for a quick resolution. Appellant did not raise the constitutionality issue and accepted the benefits of its agreement and the commission's order allowing the transfer of ownership of its shares in the track. Even if the Rule's unconstitutionality was beyond the agency's power to resolve, exhaustion of remedies could have been required; administrative action may have resolved the case on other grounds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

Baxter v. State


No. 03A04-0710-CR-596


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


891 N.E.2d 110; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1671



July 30, 2008, Decided 
July 30, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE BARTHOLOMEW CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Stephen R. Heimann, Judge. Cause No. 03C01-0606-FD-1109.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the trial court's denial of defendant's motions to declare the statutes criminalizing improper disposal of a dead animal and to suppress evidence, the Bartholomew Circuit Court (Indiana) acquitted defendant of an animal neglect charge and convicted defendant of four counts of Class D felony failure to properly dispose of a dead animal and twelve counts of Class B misdemeanor neglect of an animal. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the statutes criminalizing failure to properly dispose of a dead animal were unconstitutionally vague. The appellate court disagreed, finding that former Ind. Code § 15-2.1-16-20 (2006) (now Ind. Code § 15-17-11-20 (2008)) and former Ind. Code § 15-2.1-21-19 (now Ind. Code § 15-17-18-9 (2008)) could easily be read together as making the knowing or intentional failure to properly dispose of a dead animal a Class D felony and thus the statutes were not unconstitutionally vague. The appellate court also concluded that it was wholly appropriate for animal control to intervene for the limited purpose of opposing defendant's request to sell the animals, in clear contravention of Ind. Code § 35-46-3-6. As for the entry onto the defendant's property, the appellate court found no violation of Ind. Const. art. I, § 11, noting that, inter alia, the live malnourished horses were in plain view as officers approached defendant's house while following up on a tip. The appellate court did find, however, that the State failed to present evidence as to the cause of death of the four dead horses to support the charges involving those animals.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for four counts of Class B misdemeanor neglect of an animal were reversed. The judgment of the trial court was otherwise affirmed.

Herbert v. State


No. 79A04-0712-CR-748


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


891 N.E.2d 67; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1607



July 25, 2008, Decided 
July 25, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Herbert v. State, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 105 (Ind., Jan. 30, 2009)
Post-conviction relief denied at Herbert v. State, 2011 Ind. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1235 (Ind. Ct. App., Sept. 6, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE TIPPECANOE SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Donald C. Johnson, Judge. Cause No. 79D01-0701-FA-3.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed after being convicted in the Tippecanoe Superior Court (Indiana) on charges of dealing in cocaine, dealing in marijuana, and maintaining a common nuisance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's vehicle was stopped based on a police officer's belief that the tinted windows on the vehicle violated Ind. Code § 9-19-19-4(c). During the stop, the officer asked defendant if he had any illegal narcotics or contraband. After being told a narcotics detection canine had been called, defendant admitted to having marijuana under the driver's seat. The canine confirmed the presence of contraband, defendant was arrested, and during a search at the county jail he was found to also be in possession of cocaine. Defendant argued that the evidence should have been suppressed. The appellate court disagreed, retreating from its decision in Washington and instead following the approach set out by the dissent to that opinion. The questions asked by the officer regarding defendant's possession of narcotics or contraband did not violate Ind. Const. art. 1, § 11 so long as the questions did not materially extend the traffic stop. Defendant did not raise such a contention. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in declining to admit defendant's evidence regarding the tint on his windows. Defendant was never charged with a violation of § 9-19-19-4(c) so the evidence was irrelevant.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed.

Weideman v. State


No. 87A01-0801-CR-51


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


890 N.E.2d 28; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1509



July 16, 2008, Decided 
July 16, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE WARRICK SUPERIOR COURT NO. 1. The Honorable Keith A. Meier, Judge. Cause No. 87D01-0608-CM-206.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial, defendant was found guilty of public nudity, as a Class B misdemeanor. The Warrick Superior Court No. 1 (Indiana) sentenced defendant to 180 days in the Warrick County Security Center, but suspended that sentence and ordered defendant to serve one year of probation. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the public nudity statute, Ind. Code § 35-45-4-1.5(c), was void for vagueness because the term "public place" was ambiguous or vague such that a reasonable person would not be apprised that he could not be nude under the cover of darkness in the front yard of his private residence. Although the appellate court did not find the term ambiguous, the appellate court agreed with defendant that he was not standing in a public place. However, the appellate court concluded that defendant was visibly nude to persons in a public place, defendant's front yard, in violation of the statute. Defendant also contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction because no witness testified they saw his genitalia, pubic area, or buttocks, and he had no specific intent to be seen. The appellate court concluded that the evidence was insufficient to support public nudity, as a Class B misdemeanor, but supported public nudity, as a Class C misdemeanor. The evidence included the testimony of an officer that defendant dropped and rolled when lights were turned on him and defendant's testimony he did not want to be seen.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to enter a judgment convicting defendant of public nudity, as a Class C misdemeanor, and to resentence defendant.

Boyd v. State

889 N.E.2d 321; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1339



June 27, 2008, Decided 
June 27, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Boyd v. State, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 860 (Ind., Sept. 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE HUNTINGTON SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Jeffrey R. Heffelfinger, Judge. Cause No. 35D01-0707-FD-153.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for domestic battery as a class D felony by the Huntington Superior Court (Indiana). Defendant argued that the domestic battery statute, Ind. Code § 35-42-2-1.3(b)(2) was unconstitutionally vague as applied in his case and the evidence was insufficient to sustain his conviction for domestic battery as a class D felony as the State failed to prove that he knew a child might be able to see or hear the offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the victim, who had a four-year relationship, had an argument. They continued the argument in their bedroom, which they shared with their child. Defendant "smacked" the victim two times on the face while the victim was on the bed. The child was sleeping in her bed that was about six feet away from defendant and the victim's bed. While the victim was still on the bed, defendant put his arm around the victim's face, and the victim bit defendant. The appellate court held that § 35-42-2-1.3(b)(2) was constitutional and was not vague in defendant's case. Individuals of ordinary intelligence would comprehend § 35-42-2-1.3(b)(2) adequately to inform them of the proscribed conduct and that the statute is not unconstitutionally vague. The child did not have to sense the battery under § 35-42-2-1.3(b)(2). Evidence of probative value existed from which the trial court could have found that the child might have been able to see or hear the offense and that defendant was guilty of domestic battery as a class D felony.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

463 of 1119 DOCUMENTS


Lindsey v. State


No. 32A01-0802-PC-77


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


888 N.E.2d 319; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1254



June 13, 2008, Decided 
June 13, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Lindsey v. State, 898 N.E.2d 1219, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 733 (Ind., 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Lindsey v. Finnan, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 61489 (S.D. Ind., July 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE HENDRICKS SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Karen M. Love, Judge. Cause No. 32D03-0607-PC-4.
Lindsey v. State, 839 N.E.2d 267, 2005 Ind. App. LEXIS 2248 (Ind. Ct. App., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate was convicted of felony murder and robbery, and was sentenced to 55 years' for the murder conviction and 30 years' for the robbery conviction, to be served consecutively. His conviction and sentences were affirmed on appeal, but the robbery conviction and sentence were reversed by the state's highest court. The inmate appealed the denial by the Hendricks Superior Court (Indiana) of his petition for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the inmate had waived his claim that the Indiana Penal Code of 1977, Ind. Code §§ 35-41-1-1 to 50-8-1, was based on "vindictive justice" and was contrary to Ind. Const. art. I, § 18 as he did not raise it either during his trial or on direct appeal. The issue was available to him at those times, and the inmate did not allege that he was denied the effective assistance of counsel. The inmate's claim that he was permitted to raise the issue for the first time in his petition for post-conviction relief because it was fundamental error under Ind. R.P. Post-Conviction Remedies 1 was rejected as it relied on Propes, which had been clearly--if not expressly--overruled. A petitioner in a post-conviction proceeding could allege a claim of fundamental error only when asserting either: (1) deprivation of the Sixth Amendment right to effective assistance of counsel, or (2) an issue demonstrably unavailable to the petitioner at the time of his or her trial and direct appeal. Neither of those circumstances was present here.


OUTCOME: The post-conviction court's denial of the inmate's petition for relief was affirmed.

H.R. v. R.C. (In re D.C.)


No. 22A01-0709-CV-425


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


887 N.E.2d 950; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1160



May 29, 2008, Decided 
May 29, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE FLOYD CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable J. Terrence Cody, Judge. Cause No. 22C01-0501-AD-2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant biological mother sought review of an order by the Floyd Circuit Court (Indiana) which denied her motion to set aside an adoption decree in favor of appellee adoptive mother on the basis that Ind. Code § 31-19-14-4 barred her challenge as untimely. The biological mother claimed that the adoption decree, which was entered without notice to her, was void for lack of personal jurisdiction and violated her due process rights.


OVERVIEW: The biological mother lost her place of residence. The trial court subsequently awarded custody of the child to the father. The biological mother paid child support. The father then married the adoptive mother. The adoptive mother petitioned the court to adopt the child. At the time of the adoption petition, the biological mother was over $ 14,000 in arrears on her support obligation and had not contacted the child in over a year. The biological mother did not receive notice of the adoption proceedings. The adoptive mother and father continued to receive child support payments from the biological mother. After the biological mother discovered that the child had been adopted, she filed her motion for relief from judgment alleging, inter alia, that the judgment was void for lack of personal service. The appellate court noted that the trial court determined that a diligent search would have uncovered the biological mother's actual address. Accordingly, the trial court did not have personal jurisdiction over the biological mother, and the adoption proceedings terminating her parental rights were therefore void. Ind. Code § 31-19-14-4 created an unconstitutional due process violation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed. The matter was remanded for further proceedings.

State v. Pollard


No. 05A02-0707-CR-640


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


886 N.E.2d 69; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1016



May 13, 2008, Decided 
May 13, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Polland, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1596 (Ind. Ct. App., July 10, 2008)
Transfer granted by, Vacated by State v. Pollard, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 869 (Ind., June 30, 2009)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Pollard, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 511 (Ind., June 30, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE BLACKFORD SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable John W. Forcum, Judge. Cause No. 05D01-0701-FD-047.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Indiana charged defendant with Class D felony sex offender residency offense in violation of Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11, the Indiana residency statute. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss. The Blackford Superior Court (Indiana) granted the motion to dismiss. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: When defendant was previously convicted of committing a sex-related offense against a child, defendant owned and resided in a house that was within 1000 feet of a school property, a youth program center, or a public park. Afterwards, Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11, which prohibited such a residency, was enacted. Defendant then continued to reside in his house after the statute went into effect. The trial court ruled that Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11 violated the ex post facto prohibition contained in Ind. Const. art. I, § 24 as applied to defendant because defendant had an ownership interest in his residence for years before Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11 came into effect. Furthermore, defendant was not required to vacate his residence as part of his sentence or as a consequence of his conviction. On appeal, the court found that the trial court did not err when it found that Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11 violated Ind. Const. art. I, § 24, as applied to defendant, because Ind. Code § 35-42-4-11 was an ex post facto law as applied to a person in defendant's circumstances.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

466 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Colen v. Ohio County


No. 58A01-0709-CV-434


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


890 N.E.2d 1; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1912



May 8, 2008, Decided 
May 8, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported at Colen v. Ohio County, 886 N.E.2d 118, 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 2327 (Ind. Ct. App., May 8, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE OHIO CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable Carl Taul, Special Judge. Cause No. 58C01-0612-MI-08.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former county sheriff filed suit to recover back pay. He challenged a decision of the Ohio Circuit Court (Indiana), which granted the Ind. R. Trial P. 12(B)(6) motion of appellees, a county and its officers. He claimed entitlement to a minimum salary under Ind. Code § 36-2-13-2.8 and that the payment of a lower salary under Ind. Code § 36-2-5-3 violated Ind. Const. art. 3, § 1, the separation of powers provision.


OVERVIEW: The sheriff argued that the county had to pay him under one of the two statutes specifically addressing sheriffs' salaries. The county claimed that it was entitled to pay the sheriff under the statute applying to the pay of county officers and employees in general. The resolution of the issue depended on interpreting Ind. Code §§ 36-2-5-3, 36-2-13-2.5 and 36-2-13-2.8. The court agreed with the county that §§ 36-2-13-2.5 and 36-2-13-2.8 were not the exclusive methods of determining a sheriff's salary, and that it could pay its sheriff under § 36-2-5-3. Both §§ 36-2-13-2.5 and 36-2-13-2.8 used the term "may," and the phrase in § 36-2-13-2.8, "any other form of compensation, including a salary contract," indicated that the third method under § 36-2-5-3 of compensating the sheriff was authorized. The court concluded that the trial court properly granted the county's motion to dismiss. The county was entitled to pay the sheriff under Ind. Code § 36-2-5-3, and that procedure was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

Filice v. State


No. 49A02-0707-CR-591


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


886 N.E.2d 24; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 1152



May 7, 2008, Decided 
May 7, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer denied by Filice v. State, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 636 (Ind., July 10, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Robert Altice, Judge. Cause No. 49G02-0601-FB-10753.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) convicted defendant of criminal deviate conduct and attempted rape, violations of Ind. Code §§ 35-42-4-2 and 35-41-5-1. Defendant appealed the convictions and the sentences, arguing that his motion to dismiss the attempted rape charge should have been granted on the ground that Ind. Code § 35-42-4-1(a)(2) was unconstitutionally vague under U.S. Const. amend. V and Ind. Const. art. I, §§ 12, 13.


OVERVIEW: In a case of first impression, the court considered the constitutionality of Ind. Code § 35-42-4-1(a)(2) and held that, because the statute required that a victim be unaware that a sexual act was occurring, and Rohypnol could result in a victim's outwardly-apparent state of unawareness, the statute adequately informed an individual of ordinary intelligence that sexual intercourse with an individual in a Rohypnol-induced state of unawareness was proscribed. An exhibit that was the test results of the victim's urine sample was properly admitted because it was relevant to show her state of awareness. Because the State did not speculate that defendant administered the drug to her, the exhibit's probative value was not substantially outweighed by a danger of prejudice. There was sufficient evidence for a jury to conclude that the victim was unaware that the sexual act was occurring and that defendant used force to compel her to engage in deviate sexual conduct. While the offenses showed a dark side of defendant's personality, his 34 years as a law-abiding citizen and his position as a contributing member of society were compelling. A downward departure was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as to the convictions, reversed as to the sentences imposed, and remanded the case with instructions to vacate the sentence and impose a term of eight years imprisonment for each conviction, with the sentences to run concurrently, for an aggregate term of eight years imprisonment.

Bonner v. Daniels


No. 49A02-0702-CV-188


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


885 N.E.2d 673; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 959



May 2, 2008, Decided 
May 2, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer granted by Bonner v. Daniels, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 917 (Ind., Sept. 24, 2008)
Superseded by Bonner v. Daniels, 2009 Ind. LEXIS 472 (Ind., June 2, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. CIVIL DIVISION, ROOM 1. The Honorable Cale J. Bradford, Judge. Cause No. 49D01-0604-PL-16414.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant students, by their parents, filed a class action suit against appellees, the state officials and the Indiana State Board of Education, in the Marion Superior Court (Indiana), for a declaration under Ind. Code § 34-14-1-1 et seq. that Indiana's system of financing schools violated the Education Clause of Ind. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The trial court granted the state officials' and the Board's motion to dismiss. The students appealed.


OVERVIEW: The students contended that the State of Indiana's financing of school corporations was insufficient to provide the students, and the members of the class, with a quality public education that would prepare them to function in a complex society, and to compete successfully with their peers for productive employment and opportunities for higher education. On appeal, the court found that the students properly brought the declaratory judgment action because, accepting all the allegations in the complaint as true, the students presented the court with a question to define the students' legal rights under the Education Clause. Moreover, in line with a declaratory action's purpose, pursuant to Ind. Code §§ 34-14-1-1 and 34-14-1-2, the court's response would afford relief from uncertainty and would serve a useful purpose. In addition, the court found that the Education Clause, encapsulated in Ind. Const. art. VIII, § 1, provided Indiana's children with the right to a public education, as envisioned by the framers of the Constitution. Thus, the students could bring their claim because the Education Clause was subject to judicial enforcement.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.


 

469 of 1119 DOCUMENTS



Johnson v. Blackwell


No. 49A02-0709-CV-759


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


885 N.E.2d 25; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 876



April 28, 2008, Decided 
April 28, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable John Hanley, Judge. Cause No. 49D11-0611-CT-047271.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arrestee filed an action against defendants, two detectives, a police department, and a sheriff's department, in which he alleged civil rights violations, false imprisonment/false arrest, wrongful infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy by intrusion. The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) granted defendants' motion to dismiss on the ground that the suit was time-barred under Ind. Code § 34-11-2-4. Plaintiff appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that all of plaintiff's claims were based upon the search of plaintiff's home and his arrest, both of which occurred on February 27, 2003. Under a U.S. Supreme Court holding, a limitations period began to run when a false imprisonment ended. Because false imprisonment consisted of detention without legal process, a false imprisonment ended once the victim became held pursuant to legal process. Thus, plaintiff's cause of action for false imprisonment/arrest accrued when he was bound over for trial in March 2003. His other causes of action accrued when his house was searched and he was arrested. Thus, because plaintiff did not file his complaint until 2006, it was barred by the two-year statute of limitations. The continuing wrong doctrine did not toll the limitations period, because plaintiff, who filed a motion to suppress less than a month after his arrest, was immediately aware of the facts concerning the search and the arrest. Fraudulent concealment under Ind. Code § 34-11-5-1 did not apply either. Plaintiff was present during the entire encounter on February 27. He thus had actual knowledge of the facts and was equipped to challenge his unlawful arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the complaint.

Schaffer v. Schaffer


No. 22A04-0709-CV-513


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


884 N.E.2d 423; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 871



April 23, 2008, Decided 
April 23, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE FLOYD CIRCUIT COURT. The Honorable J. Terrence Cody, Judge. Cause No. 22C01-0406-DR-162.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee stepfather filed a petition in the Floyd Circuit Court (Indiana), to modify child visitation. Appellant mother filed a response in which she requested that the court terminate the stepfather's visitation rights because he was not the child's biological father. The trial court entered an order reducing the stepfather's visitation and denying the mother's request for termination of the stepfather's visitation rights. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The stepfather and the mother had a child while they were married. They both knew that the stepfather was not the biological father of the child. When the parties divorced, the mother was awarded sole custody of the child and the stepfather was awarded visitation because of his custodial relationship with the child and because visitation was in the child's best interests. The mother argued that the trial court violated the mother's fundamental right under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to make decisions concerning the care, custody, and control of her child. She maintained that she should have been afforded a parental presumption that her decisions concerning third party visitation with her child were in her child's best interests and should have been accorded special weight. On appeal, the court found that the parental presumption applied to initial visitation proceedings, but not to the modification of visitation. Thus, the trial court's decision that it was not in the child's best interests to terminate the stepfather's visitation was not clearly erroneous as the mother failed to prove that it was in the best interests of her child to terminate visitation.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

Prime Mortg. USA, Inc. v. Nichols


No. 49A04-0610-CV-586


COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA


885 N.E.2d 628; 2008 Ind. App. LEXIS 872



April 23, 2008, Decided 
April 23, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Transfer dismissed by Fifth Third Bank v. Nichols, 2008 Ind. LEXIS 895 (Ind., Sept. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM THE MARION SUPERIOR COURT. The Honorable Kenneth H. Johnson, Judge. Cause No. 49D02-0105-CP-825.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) defaulted defendants, a company and president, as a penalty for discovery violations after plaintiff shareholder sued them for breach of fiduciary duty, statutory unpaid wages, and forgery. It then awarded her treble damages under the Crime Victims Statute, as well as attorney fees. In a supplemental proceeding, it ordered garnishment on the president's life insurance policy. The company and president appealed.


OVERVIEW: The president and the shareholder formed the company, and later each controlled 100 shares of company stock. When the business relationship between them deteriorated, the shareholder sought to sell her 50 percent interest. In a subsequent lawsuit, the president produced a forged document that claimed the shareholder had authorized issuance of stock to two other people, and, thus, she did not have a 50 percent interest. The president and the company produced little in the way of discovery responses. As a discovery sanction, the trial court entered default judgments against them. It then awarded a sum to the shareholder for her 50 percent ownership, which it trebled under the Crime Victim's Statute, Ind. Code § 34-24-3-1, due to the forgery, unpaid wages, which were also trebled, and attorney fees. The appellate court found that (1) the company could not be held liable for breach of fiduciary duty because that claim was against the president, (2) further proceedings were required to determine whether any exemption from garnishment on the president's life insurance policy was permitted, (3) the president and the company's extreme misconduct warranted the default judgment sanction.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment against the company on the shareholder's breach of fiduciary duty claim against the company. It also remanded the case to the trial court to determine whether any of the president's life insurance policy was exempt from execution. Otherwise, the appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

COURT OF APPEALS OF INDIANA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Marion Superior Court (Indiana) entered a judgment that granted appellee state attorney general's petition to enforce a civil investigative demand against appellant foreign corporation. The foreign corporation appealed.


OVERVIEW: The foreign corporation was an Ohio corporation offering and selling waterproofing franchises. It had several Indiana franchisees. It admittedly operated in Indiana in violation of Indiana law because it had not filed a Uniform Franchise Registration Application. The state attorney general then received consumer complaints about the foreign corporation. Pursuant to Ind. Three weeks later, the foreign corporation moved to dismiss the petition to enforce and claimed the trial court lacked personal jurisdiction over it. The trial court later summarily granted the petition to enforce. The appellate court found that (1) the foreign corporation could not cite its violation of the law, the lack of registration, as a jurisdictional escape hatch and, (2) in any event, the foreign corporation had significant contacts with Indiana such that it was not constitutionally impermissible to exercise personal jurisdiction over it.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment.
